$b_{\mathrm{Experimental}}$ error in $K_{\mathbf{H}}$ is approximately $\pm 5\%$

![](images/64a74904d6304264025dc105740b5fb2a653073d2cc50fa61b40ef0d280f2691.jpg)  
Fig. 8.1. Henry's law solubility of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ vs activity of LiF in molten LiF-BeF $_2$ at 500, 600, and $700^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

at this temperature and the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ equilibrium pressure, the reactivity loss would be $15.5\%$ ; this number is about eight times greater than a $2\%$ reactivity loss which assures subcriticality. Thus fairly low pressures of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ in equilibrium with MSBR fuel salt are sufficient for

positive shutdown of the reactor. To obtain some idea about the rate of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ dissolution in fuel salt, we permitted $\mathrm{BF}_3$ to flow into the vapor space of an evacuated vessel containing $2\mathrm{kg}$ of MSBR fuel solvent at $585^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Except for possible convection currents, the salt remained quiescent during the gas inflow. Under a $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressure of 28 psig, the initial rate of dissolution was $0.63\times 10^{-6}$ mole of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ per minute per cubic centimeter of salt. This rate of dissolution corresponds to a B/U atom ratio of 0.004 per minute, and if this rate prevailed in a reactor, subcriticality would have occurred in $10\mathrm{min}$ . However, this time interval refers to a stagnant salt melt and a relatively low driving force of 28 psig of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ . Very simple improvement in $\mathrm{BF}_3$ injection, together with circulation of the salt, would have greatly shortened the time to subcriticality. These considerations suggest that $\mathrm{BF}_3$ gas at moderate pressures can be effective in a backup scram system in the MSBR. The $\mathrm{BF}_3$ could be subsequently removed from the salt by first sparging with helium, followed by neutronically "burning" any boron residue in the salt.

The measurements of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ solubility in MSBR fuel solvent are also useful in estimating certain effects of mixing fluoroborate coolant and MSBR fuel salt. If fluoroborate coolant were to leak into the MSBR fuel circuit, the $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ in the coolant would decompose to NaF and $\mathrm{BF}_3$ ; the former would dissolve in the salt, while the latter would distribute between the salt and the vapor space. The equilibrium distribution can be calculated from the following equations:

$$
n _ {T} = n _ {g} + n _ {s}, \tag {1}
$$

$$
\frac {n _ {s}}{n _ {g}} = \frac {R T K _ {H}}{\bar {V} _ {M}} \frac {V _ {s}}{V _ {g}}, \tag {2}
$$

where $n_T$ is the total number of moles of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ leaking in from the coolant; $n_g$ and $n_s$ are the number of moles of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ in the vapor space and in the salt respectively; $R$ is the gas constant; $\overline{V}_M$ is the molar volume of the salt in the fuel circuit; $T$ is the temperature in degrees Kelvin; $V_s$ and $V_g$ are the volumes of salt and vapor space in the fuel circuit (to a good approximation, $V_g / V_s$ is the void fraction in the salt); and $K_{\mathrm{H}}$ is Henry's law constant in moles of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ per mole of salt per atmosphere of $\mathrm{BF}_3$ , almost the same as the units of $K_{\mathrm{H}}$ in Table 8.2. Equation (2) is readily derived from the ideal gas law in conjunction with simple dimensional considerations.

A simple application of the above equations involves the case of a low inleakage such that $K_{\mathrm{H}}$ is not very different from the $K_{\mathrm{H}}$ of the fuel itself. Assume $T = 950^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ $(1250^{\circ}\mathrm{F})$ and a void fraction of 0.01; also $R = 82.054~\mathrm{cm}^3\cdot \mathrm{atm} / (^{\circ}\mathrm{K}\cdot \mathrm{mole})$ and $\overline{V}_s = 19.5~\mathrm{cm}^3 /\mathrm{mole}$ . Solving Eq. (2),

$$
\begin{array}{l} \frac {n _ {s}}{n _ {g}} = \frac {8 2 . 0 5 4 \times 9 5 0}{1 9 . 5} \exp (- 1 4. 9 5) \\ \times \exp (7 6 6 7 / 9 5 0) \times \frac {1}{0 . 0 1} = 4 1 1. \\ \end{array}
$$

The magnitudes of $n_{s}$ and $n_{g}$ would then be calculated from Eq. (1).

# 8.2 FREE ENERGIES OF FORMATION OF $\mathrm{NaFeF}_3$ AND $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ ; THEIR RELATIONSHIP TO THE CORROSION OF HASTELLOY N BY FLUOROBORATES

B. F. Hitch C. E. Bamberger C. F. Baes, Jr.

The double salts $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ and $\mathrm{NaFeF}_3$ have been shown to be stable, relatively insoluble compounds in molten $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ mixtures. A knowledge of their formation free energies would permit an estimate of the equilibrium position of such corrosion reactions as

$$
\begin{array}{l} 2 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {N i} (\text {H a s t l e l o y N}) \\ + \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a N i F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) + \mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}), \tag {1} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} 2 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {F e} (\text {H a s t l e l o y N}) \\ + \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a F e F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) + \mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}). \tag {2} \\ \end{array}
$$

The former reaction should correspond to the most oxidizing condition which Hastelloy N can tolerate.

The present work describes the experimental determination of $\Delta G^f$ of $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ and of $\mathrm{NaFeF}_3$ based on measurements of the equilibria

$$
\mathrm {N a N i F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {N i F} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}), K _ {3 a} = a _ {\mathrm {N a F}}, \tag {3a}
$$

$$
\mathrm {N a F e F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {F e F} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}), K _ {3 b} = a _ {\mathrm {N a F}}, \tag {3b}
$$

where $a_{\mathrm{NaF}}$ is the activity of NaF, and (c) and (d) represent, respectively, the crystalline and dissolved state. The experimental procedure consisted in equilibrating at various temperatures the solids $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ and $\mathrm{NaNiF_3}$ or $\mathrm{FeF_2}$ and $\mathrm{NaFeF_3}$ with molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ while accurately measuring the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressures. Since the solids are not very soluble in molten $\mathrm{NaBF_4 + NaF}$ , the liquid phase may be treated as a simple binary mixture, and thus the $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressures measured can be used to calculate the NaF content of the melt corresponding to equilibria $(3a)$ and $(3b)$ above. The $\mathrm{NaBF_4 - NaFeF_3 - FeF_2}$ mixture was contained in a $1^{1/2}$ -in. nickel vessel fitted with a copper liner; the mixture with niobium compounds did not have the liner. Each reaction vessel was equipped with a thermocouple well and connected directly to a mercury manometer and, through a Hoke 413 valve, to a vacuum pump. All tube connections were either silver soldered or welded.

Typically (where M is either niobium or iron), 0.3 mole of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ , 0.1 mole of $\mathrm{MF}_2$ , and 0.06 mole of $\mathrm{NaMF}_3$ were loaded into the reaction vessel. The $\mathrm{NaMF}_3$ compounds were prepared by heating stoichiometric amounts of NaF and $\mathrm{MF}_2$ with a small amount of $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ at $800^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ under argon. X-ray diffraction analysis of the product indicated it to be more than $95\%$ $\mathrm{NaMF}_3$ . After loading, the reaction vessel was sealed and flushed with argon several times. The system was then evacuated for several hours at room temperature; no detectable leaks were found. The reaction vessel was then heated to $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ and cooled to room temperature. A residual gas pressure of 50 and $80~\mathrm{mmHg}$ remained (with iron and niobium compounds respectively), due probably to unreacted $\mathrm{BF}_3$ and traces of water which had reacted to produce HF. Subsequently, the system was evacuated once more to a low pressure (1 to $10\mu$ ) to remove any residual gas. All the data presented here (Fig. 8.2) are from measurements made during the latter runs. At the end of each equilibration the solids were analyzed by x-ray diffraction, which confirmed the presence of $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ and $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ and of $\mathrm{FeF}_2$ and $\mathrm{NaNFeF}_3$ .

![](images/2b47823bb18c8e2e6f3159b253bbcbc2ccc940101fbb46357bd2adb741115c62.jpg)  
Fig. 8.2. $\mathbf{BF}_3$ pressures generated by NaF-NaBF $_4$ melts saturated with (1) $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ -NaNiF $_3$ and (2) $\mathrm{FeF}_2$ -NaFeF $_3$ . The lines were calculated by a least-squares fit of the data according to Eqs. (6), (7), and (9) or (10).

The activities of $\mathrm{NaF}, a_{\mathrm{NaF}}$ , were calculated from the measured $\mathrm{BF}_3$ pressures by means of the equilibrium constant for the reaction

$$
\mathrm {N a B F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {B F} _ {3} (\mathrm {g}), \tag {4}
$$

$$
K _ {4} = P _ {\mathrm {B F} _ {3}} \frac {a _ {\mathrm {N a F}}}{a _ {\mathrm {N a B F} _ {4}}} ,
$$

which has been measured by Cantor<sup>7</sup> as

$$
\log K _ {4} = \left[ 5. 7 7 2 - 6. 5 1 3 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 0 4. \tag {5}
$$

Introducing mole fractions $(X)$ and activity coefficients $(\gamma)$ into the equilibrium constant expression and solving for $a_{\mathrm{NaF}}$ , we obtain

$$
a _ {\mathrm {N a F}} = \frac {1}{P _ {\mathrm {B F} _ {3}} / K _ {4} \gamma_ {\mathrm {N a B F} _ {4}} + 1 / \gamma_ {\mathrm {N a F}}}. \tag {6}
$$

In the present measurements, wherein the melt was always $>97$ mole $\% \mathrm{NaBF}_4$ , Cantor's results indicate that $\gamma_{\mathrm{NaBF}_4}$ is very nearly 1.0 and $\gamma_{\mathrm{NaF}}$ is 1.3 at

$1000^{\circ}\mathrm{K}$ . Assuming $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ mixtures to have an ideal entropy of mixing, we may then estimate

$$
\log \gamma_ {\mathrm {N a F}} = \left(\frac {1 1 3 . 9}{T}\right) \pm 0. 0 4. \tag {7}
$$

The mole fractions of NaF at each equilibration temperature [calculated from Eqs. (6) and (7)] are plotted in Fig. 8.3.

The smooth curves shown in Figs. 8.2 and 8.3 were generated by assuming that $\log K_{3a}$ and $\log K_{3b}$ vary linearly with $1 / T(^{\circ}\mathrm{K})$ , that is, that

$$
\log a _ {\mathrm {N a F}} = a + b \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right). \tag {8}
$$

First, the more numerous data from the equilibrium with $\mathrm{NiF}_2\text{-NaNiF}_3$ were fitted by least squares, and then assuming that the entropies of reactions (3a) and (3b) are the same, the $P_{\mathrm{BF}_3}$ data from the equilibrium with $\mathrm{FeF}_2\text{-NaFeF}_3$ were fitted by least squares, using the value of $a$ determined for equilibrium (3a). The resulting expressions for the equilibrium constants and free energies for reactions (3a) and (3b) are

$$
\log K _ {3 a} = \left[ 0. 5 8 - 2. 1 1 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 0 4, \tag {9}
$$

$$
\log K _ {3 b} = \left[ 0. 5 8 - 1. 9 3 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 0 4, \tag {10}
$$

$$
\Delta G [ \text {r e a c t i o n} (3 a) ] = [ 9. 6 6 - 2. 6 5 (T / 1 0 ^ {3}) ] \pm 0. 2, (9 ^ {\prime})
$$

$$
\Delta G [ \text {r e a c t i o n} (3 b) ] = [ 8. 8 1 - 2. 6 5 (T / 1 0 ^ {3}) ] \pm 0. 2. (1 0 ^ {\prime})
$$

These results were then used to calculate the free energies of formation of $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ and $\mathrm{NaFeF}_3$ as follows:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N a N i F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) \right] = \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {l}) \right] \\ + \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N i F} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) \right] - \Delta G (\text {r e a c t i o n} 3 a), \tag {11} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N a F e F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) \right] = \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N a F} (\mathrm {l}) \right] \\ + \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {F e F} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) \right] - \Delta G (\text {r e a c t i o n} 3 b). \tag {12} \\ \end{array}
$$

![](images/bd5dbea164c8e35bc9595cf8c8988c4c0205a4192638eef98faef8dd24e1f2a6.jpg)  
Fig. 8.3. NaF mole fractions in equilibrium with $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ saturated with (1) $\mathrm{NiF}_2$ - $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ and (2) $\mathrm{FeF}_2$ - $\mathrm{NaFeF}_3$ . The lines were calculated from Eqs. (7) and (9) or (10).

The formation free energies of $\mathrm{NiF}_2(c)$ and of $\mathrm{FeF}_2(c)$ are known from the measurements of Blood:9

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N i F} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) \right] = [ - 1 5 6. 3 3 \\ \left. + 3 7. 6 5 \left(T / 1 0 ^ {3}\right) \right] \pm 0. 9, \tag {13} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {F e F} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) \right] = [ - 1 6 8. 6 2 \\ \left. + 3 2. 9 8 \left(T / 1 0 ^ {3}\right) \right] \pm 0. 9. \tag {14} \\ \end{array}
$$

The formation free energy of liquid NaF was obtained from the JANAF tables:10

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G ^ {f} [ \mathrm {N a F} (1) ] = [ - 1 3 0. 3 9 \\ \left. + 1 9. 4 2 \left(T / 1 0 ^ {3}\right) \right] \pm 1. 0. \tag {15} \\ \end{array}
$$

Combining these gives the formation free energies of $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ and $\mathrm{NaFeF}_3$ :

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N a N i F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) \right] = \left[ - 2 9 6. 3 8 \right. \\ \left. + 5 9. 7 2 \left(T / 1 0 ^ {3}\right) \right] \pm 1. 4, \tag {16} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta G ^ {f} \left[ \mathrm {N a F e F} _ {3} (\mathrm {c}) \right] = [ - 3 0 7. 8 2 \\ \left. + 5 5. 0 5 \left(T / 1 0 ^ {3}\right) \right] \pm 1. 4. \tag {17} \\ \end{array}
$$

Combining Eq. (9) with the equilibrium constant for the reduction of $\mathrm{NiF}_2(\mathbf{c})$ by hydrogen, based on measurements by Blood, $^9$

$$
\mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {N i F} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}) \rightleftharpoons 2 \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {N i} ^ {0} (\mathrm {c}), \tag {18}
$$

$$
\log K _ {1 8} = \left[ 8. 6 7 - 5. 6 7 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 0 4,
$$

we obtain the equilibrium constant of reaction (1):

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log \left(P _ {\mathrm {H} _ {2}} / P _ {\mathrm {H F}} ^ {2} a _ {\mathrm {N i}} 0 X _ {\mathrm {N a F}} \gamma_ {\mathrm {N a F}}\right) \\ = \left[ - 9. 2 5 + 7. 7 8 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 0 6. \tag {19} \\ \end{array}
$$

Similarly, combining Eq. (10) with the equilibrium constant for the reduction of $\mathbf{FeF}_2(c)$ by hydrogen, based on measurements by Blood, $^9$

$$
\log K _ {2 0} = [ 6. 6 5 - 8. 3 6 (1 0 ^ {3} / T) ] \pm 0. 0 2, \tag {20}
$$

we obtain the equilibrium constant for reaction (2):

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log \left(P _ {\mathrm {H} _ {2}} / P _ {\mathrm {H F}} ^ {2} a _ {\mathrm {F e} ^ {0}} X _ {\mathrm {N a F}} \gamma_ {\mathrm {N a F}}\right) \\ = \left[ - 8. 2 3 + 1 0. 2 8 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 0 5. \tag {21} \\ \end{array}
$$

Assuming that the activities of nickel and iron in Hastelloy N are, respectively, $\sim 0.70$ and $\sim 0.05$ , and that the activity of NaF in the coolant salt is $\sim 0.08$ (for the eutectic composition), the ratios of $\mathrm{PHF}_2 / \mathrm{PH}_2$ at which nickel and iron will be oxidized, respectively, to $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ and to $\mathrm{NaFeF}_3$ are calculated as follows:

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Temperature (°C)</td><td colspan="2">Ni0/NaNiF3</td><td colspan="2">Fe0/NaFeF3</td></tr><tr><td>K19</td><td>PHF2/PH2</td><td>K20</td><td>PHF2/PH2</td></tr><tr><td>400</td><td>203.0</td><td>0.09</td><td>1.10 × 107</td><td>2.3 × 10-5</td></tr><tr><td>500</td><td>6.5</td><td>2.75</td><td>1.16 × 105</td><td>2.2 × 10-3</td></tr><tr><td>600</td><td>0.5</td><td>38.82</td><td>3.50 × 103</td><td>7.14 × 10-2</td></tr></table>

From these estimates it would appear that relatively large HF partial pressures may be used, if hydrogen is present, without significant amounts of nickel oxidation in $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ mixtures. Under similar conditions the iron in Hastelloy N is more susceptible to oxidation by HF.

The availability of formation free energies and the low solubilities of $\mathrm{NaNiF}_3$ and $\mathrm{NaFeF}_3$ in molten

$\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ mixtures suggest that these compounds would be promising materials for reference electrodes to be used in molten $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ .

# 8.3 PREPARATION OF FUSED SODIUM FLUOROBORATE FOR THE COOLANT SALT TECHNOLOGY FACILITY

F. A. Doss

W.P.Teichert

Wiley Jennings, Jr.

J. H. Shaffer

The preparation of approximately 1550 lb of the fused mixture $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF (92-8 mole %), for use in operating the Coolant Salt Technology Facility (CSTF), was begun on January 3, 1972, and completed on March 7, 1972. This production operation was conducted in the Fluoride Production Facility in six batch operations of about 258 lb each. Since the CSTF is currently under construction, its detached drain tank was positioned within the production facility for direct loading from the batch processing unit. The loaded drain tank was then moved to the CSTF for installation.

Sodium fluoroborate used in this production effort was part of a 6000-lb lot purchased from the Harshaw Chemical Company as a custom preparation. Maximum concentrations of impurities in this material were specified at levels sufficiently restrictive to facilitate the production of the fused mixture. Sodium fluoride, which was added to make the fused fluoroborate mixture, was RACS grade and did not significantly affect the total impurity concentration of the mixture. With these starting materials the production procedure was reduced effectively to the removal of water vapor by evacuation while heating the powdered salts at controlled rates up to $300^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . Although materials

specifications allowed $1000~\mathrm{ppm}$ by weight of water in the starting materials, only negligible quantities were collected in the cold trap during each run. The salt mixture was then heated beyond its melting point to $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ under a static atmosphere of argon and then sparged at 10 liters/min with $\mathrm{BF}_3$ for $10\mathrm{min}$ to ensure mixing of the two salts. Residual $\mathrm{BF}_3$ was purged from the system with argon, and a filtered sample of the salt was withdrawn for chemical analyses. The salt transfer line between the drain tank and the production vessel was connected, and the molten fluoroborate mixture was displaced into the drain tank by argon pressure. This procedure was repeated for each of the six batches of fluoroborate mixture.

The results of chemical analyses from the six batch preparations are shown in Table 8.3. The averages of these values should be representative of the total of materials transferred into the drain tank. Nickel, chromium, and iron contents of 16, 13, and $141~\mathrm{ppm}$ by weight, respectively, are well within limits specified for molten-salt systems. The average oxide content of the melt was $319~\mathrm{ppm}$ by weight and compares favorably with a specified limit of $250~\mathrm{ppm}$ in the starting material. Results of proton analyses on five of the six batches correspond to $15~\mathrm{ppm}$ by weight. This quantity, if present as the hydroxyl ion, would imply its association with $240~\mathrm{ppm}$ of the total oxide found. The average values reported for the major constituents (i.e., sodium, boron, and fluorine) differ from those calculated for the mixture by quantities no greater than the error limits of the analytical determinations. Arbitrary calculations of the salt composition in mole percent yield an average value of $95.2\%$ $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ and $4.8\%$ NaF. This implied discrepancy in the salt composition is of little consequence to the operation of the CSTF, since

Table 8.3. Results of chemical analyses of $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ -NaF (92-8 mole %) prepared for the Coolant Salt Technology Facility   

<table><tr><td rowspan="2">Batch No.</td><td colspan="3">Major constituents (wt %)</td><td colspan="5">Impurities (ppm by weight)</td></tr><tr><td>Na</td><td>B</td><td>F</td><td>Ni</td><td>Cr</td><td>Fe</td><td>O</td><td>H</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>21.2</td><td>9.89</td><td>69.3</td><td>21</td><td>38</td><td>223</td><td>275</td><td>NA</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>21.6</td><td>9.51</td><td>67.9</td><td>10</td><td>8</td><td>25</td><td>320</td><td>17</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>22.2</td><td>9.45</td><td>69.0</td><td>19</td><td>&lt;10</td><td>203</td><td>306</td><td>15</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>21.7</td><td>9.93</td><td>67.8</td><td>16</td><td>8</td><td>135</td><td>267</td><td>14</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>21.4</td><td>9.77</td><td>69.3</td><td>15</td><td>6</td><td>125</td><td>437</td><td>13</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>21.9</td><td>9.66</td><td>67.7</td><td>15</td><td>6</td><td>135</td><td>310</td><td>15</td></tr><tr><td>Average</td><td>21.67</td><td>9.70</td><td>68.5</td><td>16</td><td>13</td><td>141</td><td>319</td><td>15</td></tr><tr><td>Calculated</td><td>22.03</td><td>9.53</td><td>68.4</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table>

the actual salt composition will readily adjust to an equilibrium with the $\mathbf{BF}_3$ value of the cover gas stream.

The sodium fluoroborate mixture prepared by this production effort is considerably better, with respect to its oxide content, than previous batches produced by

generally similar procedures for loop operations. This improvement is probably the direct result of the "best effort" provided by the Harshaw Chemical Company in their preparation of the sodium fluoroborate starting material.

# 9. Protactinium Chemistry

# 9.1 OXIDE CHEMISTRY OF PROTACTINIUM IN MSBR FUEL SALT

R.G.Ross C.E.Bamberger C.F.Baes,Jr.

Studies of the precipitation of protactinium from molten $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4}$ (72-16-12 mole %), described in several previous progress reports, $^{1-3}$ have been completed, and the results are summarized here.

Under sufficiently oxidizing conditions (Fig. 9.1), a very insoluble compound of protactinium(V) is formed in which, judging from the stoichiometry of the precipitation, the O/Pa ratio is 2.5. It is thought that this compound is a fluoride addition compound, probably of LiF, because: (1) the entropy change determined for the following equilibrium is about 23 eU higher than expected for pure $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ :

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {P a O} _ {2. 5} \cdot n \mathrm {L i F} (\mathrm {c}) + ^ {5} / _ {4} \mathrm {T h F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) \\ \Leftrightarrow \mathrm {P a F} _ {5} (\mathrm {d}) + n \mathrm {L i F} (\mathrm {d}) + 5 / 4 \mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (\mathrm {c}), \tag {1} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log \left(X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {5}}\right) / \left(X _ {\mathrm {T h F} _ {4}}\right) ^ {5 / 4} \\ = \left[ 4. 4 9 - 8. 6 6 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 2. \\ \end{array}
$$

(As usual, the letters g, d, c, and ss will denote, respectively, components in the gaseous, molten fluoride, crystalline, and solid solution states.) This suggests that when the $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ phase reacts with the molten fluoride, it releases more species to the solution than would be the case for pure $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ . (2) Despite several attempts, we were unable to identify pure $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ in oxides separated from equilibrated mixtures. If the

$\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ phase does contain added fluoride, it is probably LiF, since lithium ion is the most basic cation present.

With $\mathrm{UF_4}$ in the molten fluoride at concentrations typical of an MSBR fuel ( $\sim 0.3$ mole \%), the $\mathrm{ThO_2}$ phase in reaction (1) is replaced by a solid solution $\mathrm{UO_2 - ThO_2}$ ( $\sim 95$ to $\sim 5$ mole \%):

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {P a O} _ {2. 5} \cdot n \mathrm {L i F} (c) + ^ {5} / _ {4} \mathrm {U F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) \\ \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {P a F} _ {5} (\mathrm {d}) + n \mathrm {L i F} (\mathrm {d}) + ^ {5} / _ {4} \mathrm {U O} _ {2} (\mathrm {s s}), \tag {2} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log \left(X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {5}}\right) \left(X _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} \gamma_ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}}\right) ^ {5 / 4} / \left(X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}}\right) ^ {5 / 4} \\ = [ 4. 4 9 - 5. 6 9 (1 0 ^ {3} / T) ] \pm 0. 2. \\ \end{array}
$$

The equilibrium quotient for this reaction was measured directly and found to agree well with values predicted from reaction (1) and the previously determined quotient for the $\mathrm{U}^{4+}$ - $\mathrm{Th}^{4+}$ exchange between fluoride and oxide phases (see the following sections). The resulting values indicate that under sufficiently oxidizing conditions and sufficiently low temperatures a large fraction of the protactinium in an MSBR fuel may be efficiently isolated as a pure phase without precipitation of the $\mathrm{UO}_2$ solid solution or any other phase.

Under reducing conditions, the $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ phase is solubilized as $\mathrm{PaF}_4$ :

$$
\begin{array}{l} \mathrm {P a O} _ {2. 5} \cdot n \mathrm {L i F} (\mathrm {c}) + 5 / 4 \mathrm {U F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) + 1 / 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}) \\ \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {P a F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) + n \mathrm {L i F} (\mathrm {d}) + 5 / 4 \mathrm {U O} _ {2} (\mathrm {s s}), \tag {3} \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log \left(X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {4}}\right) \left(X _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} \gamma_ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}}\right) ^ {5 / 4} \left(P _ {\mathrm {H F}}\right) / \left(X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}}\right) ^ {5 / 4} \\ \times \left(P _ {\mathrm {H} _ {2}}\right) ^ {1 / 2} = \left[ 8. 4 1 - 1 1. 4 4 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 5. \\ \end{array}
$$

Combination of reactions (2) and (3) gives the equilibrium

$$
\mathrm {H F} (\mathrm {g}) + \mathrm {P a F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {P a F} _ {5} (\mathrm {d}) + 1 / 2 \mathrm {H} _ {2} (\mathrm {g}), \tag {4}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \log \left(X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {5}}\right) \left(P _ {\mathrm {H} _ {2}}\right) ^ {1 / 2} / \left(X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {4}}\right) \left(P _ {\mathrm {H F}}\right) \\ = \left[ - 3. 9 2 + 5. 1 6 \left(1 0 ^ {3} / T\right) \right] \pm 0. 5. \\ \end{array}
$$

![](images/38134f5cc1ca7274f17620064d5ee313e3892dfe3d57255fbfaa162153119862.jpg)  
Fig. 9.1. Pourbaix diagram for protactinium species in molten $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ThF_4 - UF_4}$ (72-16-11.7-0.3 mole %) at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

This reaction is of special interest, since such reactions, involving the $\mathrm{HF} / \mathrm{H}_2$ , $\mathrm{F}^-$ couple, have been used to establish a set of $E^0$ values for various couples in MSR molten fluorides. $^4$

From the equilibria measured and other thermochemical data, the following formation heats and free energies were estimated for protactinium compounds:

<table><tr><td>Compound</td><td>ΔHf298(kcal/mole)</td><td>ΔGf700-1000(kcal/mole)</td></tr><tr><td>PaO2(c)</td><td>(-270)</td><td>-268.5 + 41.3 (T/103)</td></tr><tr><td>PaF4(d)</td><td></td><td>-469.3 + 61.1 (T/103)</td></tr><tr><td>PaF5(d)</td><td></td><td>-558.1 + 78.0 (T/103)</td></tr><tr><td>“Pa2O5(c)”</td><td>-697</td><td>-693.5 + 147.6 (T/103)</td></tr></table>

The $\Delta H_{298}^{f}$ value for $\mathrm{PaO}_2$ was estimated by interpolation from the values for $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ and $\mathrm{UO}_2$ . The values for $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ were calculated on the assumption that the $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ phase in the present system contained no fluoride. If, as we suspect, it does, then these $\Delta H^{f}$ and $\Delta G^{f}$ values are more negative than those for pure $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ .

A Pourbaix diagram, representing the behavior of protactinium in an MSBR fuel as a function of Redox potential and the oxide concentration, is shown in Fig. 9.1. The striking features are the strong dependence of

the solubility of the protactinium on the oxidation potential (i.e., the $\mathrm{U}^{4 + } / \mathrm{U}^{3 + }$ ratio) of the fuel and the very low solubility of the $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ phase. The potential of the $\mathrm{Pa}^{5 + } / \mathrm{Pa}^{4 + }$ couple will determine the maximum $\mathrm{U}^{4 + } / \mathrm{U}^{3 + }$ ratio in the fuel necessary to prevent inadvertent precipitation of protactinium oxide. It will also determine the minimum $\mathrm{U}^{4 + } / \mathrm{U}^{3 + }$ ratio in the fuel necessary to provide the desired insolubility of the $\mathrm{Pa}_2\mathrm{O}_5$ phase in a separation process. Because of the importance of the $\mathrm{Pa}^{5 + } / \mathrm{Pa}^{4 + }$ couple, it is in need of more accurate determination. Plans are being made for such measurements which will employ volumetric and/or spectrophotometric methods.

# 9.2 BINARY SOLID SOLUTIONS OF $\mathrm{PaO}_2$ AND OTHER ACTINIDE DIOXIDES AND THEIR EXCHANGE EQUILIBRIA WITH MOLTEN-SALT REACTOR FLUORIDES<sup>5</sup>

C.E.Bamberger R.G.Ross C.F.Baes,Jr.

We have previously reported $^{6}$ on measurements of the equilibrium quotient of the reaction

$$
\mathrm {T h O} _ {2} (\mathrm {s s}) + \mathrm {P a F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) \rightleftharpoons \mathrm {T h F} _ {4} (\mathrm {d}) + \mathrm {P a O} _ {2} (\mathrm {s s}), \tag {1}
$$

$$
Q _ {\mathrm {T h}} ^ {\mathrm {P a}} = X _ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}} X _ {\mathrm {T h F} _ {4}} / X _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {4}}.
$$

We found that the distribution of $\mathrm{Pa}^{4+}$ favors the oxide phase, although not as strongly as $\mathrm{U}^{4+}$ in a similar system. Additional data have been obtained from a new experiment where the amount of $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ added was significantly increased in order to decrease the ratio $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ dissolved/ $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ added. This, in turn, reduced the uncertainty in the composition of the precipitated phase when calculated by material balance. Previous and present data are shown in Table 9.1.

The values obtained for the distribution quotient $Q_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{Pa}}$ have been correlated with the previous determinations of $Q_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{U}}$ (ref. 3) and a value of $Q_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{Pu}}$ derived indirectly from previous measurements on the basis of the following considerations:

1. Hietala<sup>9</sup> has successfully accounted for the observed heats of mixing of binary alkali halide solid solutions with a common ion in terms of a simple model which calculates the displacement of the common ion in a fixed lattice of the randomly mixed counter ions. For 1 mole of solution the result is of the approximate form

$$
\Delta H _ {m} \approx C \left(\frac {d _ {2} - d _ {1}}{d _ {1}}\right) ^ {2} X _ {1} X _ {2}, \tag {2}
$$

where $d_{1}$ and $d_{2}$ are the cation-anion distances for the two pure salts. For solid solutions with the NaCl structure, $C$ is a constant. It contains no adjustable parameters, being a function only of the molar volumes and compressibilities of the pure salts.

While an analogous treatment has not yet been completed for substitutional solid solutions with the fluorite structure, such as those of the actinide dioxides, it is clear that the result will be of the same form. Hence for 1 mole of solution we may write

$$
\Delta H _ {m} \approx A \left(\frac {a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} - a _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}}{a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}}}\right) ^ {2}. X _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} X _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}, \tag {3}
$$

where $a_{\mathrm{MO}_2}$ is the lattice parameter for the pure oxide. If we assume random mixing, we obtain for the activity coefficients in the binary solid solutions

$$
\ln \gamma_ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}} = \frac {A}{R T} \left(\frac {a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} - a _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}}{a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}}}\right) ^ {2} X ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}}, \tag {4}
$$

$$
\ln \gamma_ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} = \frac {A}{R T} \left(\frac {a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} - a _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}}{a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}}}\right) ^ {2} X ^ {2} _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}. \tag {5}
$$

2. Considering now the exchange reaction (1), we would expect from electrostatic considerations that the enthalpy of reaction could be approximated by

$$
\Delta H ^ {0} = - B \left(\frac {1}{a _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}} - \frac {1}{a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}}}\right). \tag {6}
$$

Taking $\Delta S^0$ for this reaction to be zero, as has been found to be the case for the $\mathbf{U}^{4 + }\cdot \mathbf{Th}^{4 + }$ exchange, we obtain for the equilibrium constant

$$
\ln K _ {\mathrm {T h}} ^ {\mathrm {M}} = \frac {B}{R T} \left(\frac {1}{a _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}} - \frac {1}{a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}}}\right). \tag {7}
$$

Table 9.1. Equilibrium data obtained for the reaction $\mathrm{ThO}_2(\mathrm{ss}) + \mathrm{PaF}_4(\mathrm{d})\rightleftharpoons \mathrm{PaO}_2(\mathrm{ss}) + \mathrm{ThF}_4(\mathrm{d})$   

<table><tr><td>Sample</td><td>Temperature (°C)</td><td>XΣOa</td><td>XPaF4</td><td>XPaO2b</td><td>log QThPa</td><td>a(A)</td></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td>×10-3</td><td>×10-4</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A</td><td>567</td><td>0.35</td><td>4.38</td><td>(0.324)</td><td>2.12 ± 0.48</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>B</td><td>567</td><td>0.58</td><td>4.10</td><td>(0.291)</td><td>2.08 ± 0.26</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>C</td><td>567</td><td>1.08</td><td>3.40</td><td>(0.292)</td><td>2.16 ± 0.15</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>D</td><td>663</td><td>2.78</td><td>3.38</td><td>(0.167)</td><td>1.85 ± 0.13</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>E</td><td>727</td><td>2.70</td><td>4.16</td><td>(0.109)</td><td>1.55 ± 0.08</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>F</td><td>567</td><td>2.83</td><td>2.71</td><td>0.175 ± 0.02c</td><td>1.97 ± 0.06</td><td>5.5926 ± 0.0003</td></tr><tr><td rowspan="2">G</td><td rowspan="2">730</td><td rowspan="2">14.88</td><td rowspan="2">1.91</td><td>0.073 ± 0.004d</td><td>1.69 ± 0.05</td><td rowspan="2">5.5910 ± 0.0002</td></tr><tr><td>(0.050)</td><td>1.52 ± 0.05</td></tr><tr><td>H</td><td>567</td><td>8.88</td><td>0.746</td><td>(0.062)</td><td>2.02 ± 0.10</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>I</td><td>567</td><td>8.88</td><td>0.686</td><td>(0.063)</td><td>2.07 ± 0.10</td><td></td></tr></table>

$b$ Numbers in parentheses were calculated by material balance.   
${}^{c}$ Determined by x-ray fluorescence.   
$d$ Determined by gamma spectrometry.

3. Since it has been found experimentally that the ratio of activity coefficients in the fluoride phase $\gamma_{\mathrm{ThF}_4} / \gamma_{\mathrm{MF}_4}$ is a constant independent of melt composition, and may be taken as unity, $Q_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathbf{M}}$ and $K_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathbf{M}}$ are related by

$$
K _ {\mathrm {T h}} ^ {\mathrm {M}} = Q _ {\mathrm {T h}} ^ {\mathrm {M}} \gamma_ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}} / \gamma_ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}}. \tag {8}
$$

Combining Eqs. (4), (5), (7), and (8), the following expression for $Q_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathbf{M}}$ as a function of oxide composition and temperature is obtained:

$$
\begin{array}{l} \ln Q _ {\mathrm {T h}} ^ {\mathrm {M}} = \frac {B}{R T} \left(\frac {1}{a _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}} - \frac {1}{a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}}}\right) \\ + \frac {A}{R T} \left(\frac {a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} - a _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}}{a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}}}\right) ^ {2} (2 X _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}} - 1). \tag {9} \\ \end{array}
$$

This expression is compared with the measurements (normalized to $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) in Fig. 9.2. The values of $A$ and $B$ ,

$$
A = 2 4 4 0 \pm 3 0 0 \mathrm {k c a l / m o l e},
$$

$$
B = 2 6 2 0 \pm 5 3 \mathrm {k c a l}, \mathrm {A}, \mathrm {m o l e} ^ {- 1},
$$

have been chosen to reproduce the line previously generated by least squares to fit the $Q_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{U}}$ values vs $X_{\mathrm{UO}_2}$ and $T$ . As can be seen, the consistency with the other $Q_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{M}}$ values is quite satisfactory. The large uncertainty assigned to $Q_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{Pu}}$ reflects the large uncertainty in the free energy of dissolution of $\mathrm{UF_4(c)}$ in

The points are measurements in which the composition of the oxide phase was determined directly ( $\bullet$ ) or by material balance ( $\odot$ ). The lines were calculated by means of Eq. (7).

LiF-BeF $_2$ (67-33 mole %), assumed to be equal to that of $\mathrm{PuF}_4(\mathrm{c})$ , presently unknown. Values of $K_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{M}}$ , calculated from measured values of $Q_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{M}}$ by means of Eqs. (4), (5), and (8), are shown in Fig. 9.3, together with a line calculated with Eq. (7).

We hope in the future to obtain more data on $\mathrm{PuO_2 - ThO_2}$ solid solutions, probably by direct measurements of the equilibrium involving $\mathrm{PuF_4}$ . The large values of $K_{\mathrm{TTh}}^{\mathbf{Pu}}$ indicate that the equilibrium oxide phase precipitated from melts containing $\mathrm{ThF_4}$ , and sufficiently oxidized to contain appreciable amounts of $\mathrm{PuF_4}$ , should consist of nearly pure $\mathrm{PuO_2}$ .

# 9.3 TERNARY SOLID SOLUTIONS OF $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ , $\mathrm{PaO}_2$ , AND $\mathrm{UO}_2$

C.F.Baes,Jr.

In the previous section, the exchange of two tetravalent actinide cations, $\mathbf{M}^{4+}$ and $\mathbf{N}^{4+}$ , between a binary $\mathrm{MO}_2\cdot \mathrm{NO}_2$ solid solution and a molten fluoride solution containing $\mathbf{MF}_4$ and $\mathbf{NF}_4$ was discussed in terms of the exchange equilibrium constant

$$
K _ {\mathrm {N}} ^ {\mathrm {M}} = \frac {X _ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}} X _ {\mathrm {N F} _ {4}}}{X _ {\mathrm {N O} _ {2}} X _ {\mathrm {M F} _ {4}}} \cdot \frac {\gamma_ {\mathrm {M O} _ {2}}}{\gamma_ {\mathrm {N O} _ {2}}}.
$$

This constant and the activity coefficients, $\gamma_{\mathrm{MO}_2}$ and $\gamma_{\mathrm{NO}_2}$ , were (see previous section) represented by expressions [Eqs. (4), (5), and (7)] which involve the lattice parameters of the oxides, the temperature, and two empirical constants ( $A$ and $B$ ). In an MSBR fuel, three tetravalent actinide ions $-\mathrm{Th}^{4+}$ , $\mathrm{Pa}^{4+}$ , and $\mathrm{U}^{4+}$ will be present under normal conditions. The oxide phase which could be precipitated inadvertently by oxide contamination or intentionally for the purpose of fuel reprocessing will, therefore, be a ternary $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ - $\mathrm{PaO}_2$ - $\mathrm{UO}_2$ solid solution. In order to employ the equilibrium constants $K_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{Pa}}$ and $K_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{U}}$ (or $K_{\mathrm{Pa}}^{\mathrm{U}}$ , which is equal to $K_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{U}} / K_{\mathrm{Th}}^{\mathrm{Pa}}$ ) to determine the composition of this oxide phase as a function of the fuel composition, it is first necessary to estimate activity coefficients in such a ternary oxide solid solution. This was done as follows:

Again it is assumed that such a solid solution has an ideal entropy of mixing. The problem of estimating activity coefficients then reduces to estimating the heat of mixing of the three components, since

$$
R T \ln \gamma_ {i} = \frac {\delta \Delta H _ {m}}{\delta n _ {i}}. \tag {1}
$$

The heat of mixing of $n_1 + n_2 + n_3$ moles of the three oxides can be approximated by imagining that first the following binary solutions are made:

$$
n _ {1} + y _ {3} \text {a n d} n _ {2} + \left(n _ {3} - y _ {3}\right),
$$

$y_{3}$ being a portion of $n_3$ , chosen such that these two solid solutions have equal lattice parameters. Assuming that the lattice parameter of each solution varies linearly with mole fraction (Vegard's law), the value of $y_{3}$ which will yield the same lattice parameter for both solutions is given by

$$
y _ {3} = n _ {1} \cdot \frac {\left(a _ {1} - a _ {2}\right) n _ {2} + \left(a _ {1} - a _ {3}\right) n _ {3}}{\left(a _ {1} - a _ {3}\right) n _ {3} + \left(a _ {2} - a _ {3}\right) n _ {2}}, \tag {2}
$$

where the $a$ 's denote the lattice parameters of the pure oxides.

From Hietala's model it is expected that the heats of mixing for each of these solutions will be, to a good approximation,

$$
\Delta H \left[ n _ {1} + y _ {3} \right] = A \left(\frac {a _ {1} - a _ {3}}{a _ {1}}\right) ^ {2} \frac {n _ {1} y _ {3}}{n _ {1} + y _ {5}}, \tag {3}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \Delta H \left[ n _ {2} + \left(n _ {3} - y _ {3}\right) \right] \\ = A \left(\frac {a _ {2} - a _ {3}}{a _ {1}}\right) ^ {2} \frac {n _ {2} \left(n _ {3} - y _ {3}\right)}{\left[ n ^ {2} + \left(n _ {3} - y _ {3}\right) \right]}. \tag {4} \\ \end{array}
$$

Since the two binary solutions have the same lattice parameter, their heat of mixing to produce the final solution should be zero. Hence

$$
\Delta H _ {m} = \Delta H \left[ n _ {1} + y _ {3} \right] + \Delta H \left[ n _ {2} + \left(n _ {3} - y _ {3}\right) \right]. \tag {5}
$$

Substituting Eqs. (2), (3), and (4) into (5) gives

$$
\begin{array}{l} K _ {\mathrm {T h}} ^ {\mathrm {P a}} = \exp \left[ \frac {3 7 8 4}{T} \right], K _ {\mathrm {T h}} ^ {\mathrm {U}} \\ = \exp \left[ \frac {5 4 7 3}{T} \right], K _ {\mathrm {P a}} ^ {\mathrm {U}} = \exp \left[ \frac {1 6 8 9}{T} \right], \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \gamma_ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} = \exp \left\{\left(X _ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}} + X _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}}\right) \right. \\ \times \left[ \frac {3 0 7}{T} X _ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}} + \frac {6 3 3}{T} X _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} \right] - \frac {5 8}{T} X _ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}} X _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} \Bigg \}, \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \gamma_ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}} = \exp \left\{\left(X _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} + X _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}}\right) \right. \\ \left. \times \left[ \frac {3 0 7}{T} X _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} + \frac {5 8}{T} X _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} \right] - \frac {6 3 3}{T} X _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} X _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} \right\}, \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\begin{array}{l} \gamma_ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} = \exp \left\{\left(X _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} + X _ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}}\right) \right. \\ \times \left[ \frac {6 3 3}{T} X _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} + \frac {5 8}{T} X _ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}} \right] - \frac {3 0 7}{T} X _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} X _ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}} \Bigg \}. \\ \end{array}
$$

$$
\Delta H _ {m} = \frac {A}{a _ {1} ^ {2}} \left[ \frac {\left(a _ {1} - a _ {2}\right) ^ {2} n _ {1} n _ {2} + \left(a _ {1} - a _ {3}\right) ^ {2} n _ {1} n _ {3} + \left(a _ {2} - a _ {3}\right) ^ {2} n _ {2} n _ {3}}{n _ {1} + n _ {2} + n _ {3}} \right]. \tag {6}
$$

Differentiating with respect to $n_1$ , according to Eq. (1), gives

$$
\begin{array}{l} \gamma_ {1} = \exp \frac {A}{R T a _ {1} ^ {2}} \left[ \left(X _ {2} + X _ {3}\right) \left(a _ {1} - a _ {2}\right) ^ {2} X _ {2} \right. \\ \left. + \left(a _ {1} - a _ {3}\right) ^ {2} X _ {3} \right] - \left(a _ {2} - a _ {3}\right) ^ {2} X _ {2} X _ {3} ]; \tag {7} \\ \end{array}
$$

analogous expressions are obtained for the activity coefficients $\gamma_{2}$ and $\gamma_{3}$ .

Introducing the numerical values of $A$ and $B$ determined in the previous section and the lattice parameters for $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ , $\mathrm{PaO}_2$ , and $\mathrm{UO}_2$ ,

$$
A = 2 4 4 0 \mathrm {k c a l} / \mathrm {m o l e}; B = 2 6 2 0 \mathrm {k c a l}, \mathrm {A}, \mathrm {m o l e} ^ {- 1};
$$

$$
a _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} = 5. 5 9 7 \mathrm {A}; a _ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}} = 5. 5 0 9 \mathrm {A}; a _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} = 5. 4 7 0 4 \mathrm {A},
$$

we may write the following expressions for the various exchange equilibrium constants and activity coefficients for ternary $\mathrm{ThO}_2\text{-PaO}_2\text{-UO}_2$ solid solutions:

Curves representing the variation of the three activity coefficients with the composition of the oxide phase are shown in Fig. 9.4.

From these expressions we may calculate that for a salt containing

$$
X _ {\mathrm {T h F} _ {4}} = 0. 1 2, X _ {\mathrm {U F} _ {4}} = 0. 0 0 3, X _ {\mathrm {P a F} _ {4}} = 0. 0 0 0 3,
$$

the oxide phase at equilibrium with it (at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ ) will have the composition

$$
X _ {\mathrm {T h O} _ {2}} = 0. 0 3 6, X _ {\mathrm {U O} _ {2}} = 0. 9 5, X _ {\mathrm {P a O} _ {2}} = 0. 0 1 3.
$$

This example serves to show that the oxide solid solution precipitated from an MSBR fuel will contain little protactinium. Specifically, the ratio of $\mathrm{Pa} / \mathrm{U}$ in the oxide phase should be approximately one-seventh of that in the salt phase. For oxide solutions so dilute in $\mathrm{PaO}_2$ the values of $\gamma_{\mathrm{UO}_2}$ and $\gamma_{\mathrm{ThO}_2}$ (Fig. 9.4) will not be significantly different from those already determined for binary $\mathrm{ThO}_2\text{-}\mathrm{UO}_2$ solutions. The estimates of $\gamma_{\mathrm{PaO}_2}$ for low concentration of $\mathrm{PaO}_2$ , varying from 1.0 to $\sim 1.4$ , ought to be accurate enough for present purposes.

![](images/612324c3b2a28b7ffb084f50752993a3598be2a920fd0786d2a094c5460edcbd.jpg)

![](images/759f45b62fd07e008a85829f62ce51ecf74243e5a3d49bbce840a9c9cae68d39.jpg)  
Fig. 9.4. Estimated activity coefficients in ternary oxide solid solutions of $\mathrm{ThO}_2$ , $\mathrm{PaO}_2$ , and $\mathrm{UO}_2$ at $600^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ .

# 10. Development and Evaluation of Analytical Methods for Molten-Salt Reactors

A. S. Meyer

# 10.1 IN-LINE CHEMICAL ANALYSIS OF MOLTEN FLUORIDE SALT STREAMS

J.M.Dale A.S.Meyer

Automated analyses for U(III) in $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ZrF_4 - UF_4}$ (65.4-29.1-5.0-0.5 mole %) in the NCL-21 thermal convection salt loop were continued. The percent of the total uranium present as U(III) for the first 3600 hr of loop operation is shown in Figs. 10.1-10.3. These analyses were largely made by unattended operation of the computer-controlled voltammetric system described in the previous report. $^1$

The first analyses, made 70 hr after the salt was loaded into the loop, showed that the concentration of

1. J. M. Dale and A. S. Meyer, MSR Program Semiannu. Progr. Rep. Aug. 31, 1971, ORNL-4728, p. 69.

U(III) was about $0.02\%$ . The U(III) concentration started increasing as the chromium from the metal loop dissolved into the salt and reduced the U(IV). The irregular appearance of the data at 600 hr was due to large temperature fluctuations in the electrode tank. The first measurements with the shielded electrode were made at 1200 hr and are plotted as squares in Figs. 10.1 and 10.2. The shielded electrode is an assembly designed to eliminate the interference of material from the surface of the melt which apparently deposits on the electrode and changes its area and electrical characteristics. The electrode is surrounded by an open-ended nickel tube which can be periodically purged with helium to provide a clean melt surface.

At about $1250\mathrm{hr}$ it was first noted that analyses with the shielded and unshielded electrodes gave different values for the U(III) concentration. This was later

![](images/02f2245e67fd8e83c6dda208fc7ff77c10f322f38929271568caeba42ed858b2.jpg)  
Fig. 10.1. U(III) in MSRE fuel salt, 7/20/71 to 9/8/71.

shown to be an effect of the nickel tube around the shielded electrode and not to a difference in the platinum electrodes. At 2350 hr the nickel tube shield was raised out of the melt, and the measurements with the two electrodes were in agreement as shown in Fig. 10.2. Fortunately, the surface material has apparently been removed from the melt by continuous operation of the loop, and the unshielded electrodes are operating satisfactorily. It will probably be necessary to use shielded electrodes for other systems, however, and

further investigation is needed to determine the reason for the difference in the two electrodes. A possible explanation is that the difference results from a cooling of the melt by the shield tube.

During the first 3600 hr of loop operation the Hastelloy N corrosion specimens were inserted into the loop at three different times (140, 1316, and 3200 hr). Except for the first time, when the U(III)

2. J. W. Koger, Sect. 13.5.1, this report.

![](images/1345b688c4b0bbe94bff8c01ef4bf69ca1df9b04b19b6051582e3cf017caa60f.jpg)  
Fig. 10.2. U(III) in MSRE fuel salt, 9/8/71 to 10/28/71.

![](images/b85eca628da1ff0db554e74b2968a3b381cc92e73cbef4d3eab678a79e4ebfc8.jpg)  
Fig. 10.3. U(III) in MSRE fuel salt, 10/28/71 to 12/17/71.

concentration was already at a low value, the insertion of the metal specimens appeared to introduce an oxidant which caused a decrease in the U(III) concentration. Special precautions are being taken for the next specimen insertion to determine if this operation can be performed without oxidizing the U(III). At 1725 hr the shielded electrode, which had previously been removed for examination, was put back into the loop without immersing it into the salt. As shown in Fig. 10.2, the U(III) concentration decreased and then rapidly recovered, probably due to dilution effects caused by the salt flow.

The voltammetric technique will also be used to determine other melt constituents such as corrosion product ions. In the reducing melts that have been present in the loop the equilibrium concentration of $\mathrm{Fe(II)}$ and $\mathrm{Ni(II)}$ is negligible, while all of the chromium is present in the ionic form. In fuel melts, $\mathrm{Cr(II)}$ is the most difficult of the corrosion products to measure because its reduction potential is so close to that of U(IV) (see the next section of this report). One of the proposals which we have made but not demonstrated is that chromium can be determined by a stripping technique. This technique is based on the fact that metallic chromium, the reduction product of $\mathrm{Cr(II)}$ , is deposited on the electrode, whereas U(III), the reduction product of U(IV), is soluble and diffuses back into the melt. Thus if the electrode is held at a potential that is sufficiently cathodic to reduce both U(IV) and $\mathrm{Cr(II)}$ , the U(III) will rapidly approach a steady-state concentration at the electrode surface, while chromium metal is continuously deposited. When the electrode is electrolytically stripped by an anodic scan, the contribution of U(III) should be independent of reduction time, while the stripping current from the chromium metal should increase with plating time.

Figure 10.4 shows some typical stripping curves for chromium at the $100\mathrm{-ppm}$ level in the salt. Chromium was plated on the working electrodes for different lengths of time at $-360\mathrm{mV}$ vs $E_{\mathrm{EQ}}$ and then stripped from the electrode at a scan rate of $0.1\mathrm{V / sec}$ . When these stripping currents were integrated with the voltmeter and plotted vs plating time, an excellent straight-line relationship was obtained. Extrapolation to zero plating time showed that about $4.5\mathrm{mC}$ of the current, a reasonable value, was due to reoxidation of U(III) to U(IV). In theory, the slope of the curves of integrated current vs plating time should be proportional to the concentration of chromium ion in the melt. Although gradual increases in the slope of such curves which may correspond to increases in the concentration in the melt have been observed, we have

![](images/7eb2ebe66a5ee36ee228d6b0ed876f0e3a67c5ea80f24f3c001fb0acab614033.jpg)  
Fig. 10.4. Stripping curves for chromium in MSRE fuel.

not accumulated sufficient analytical data to establish the analytical validity of this technique.

In summary, the results of the ratio measurements have, in general, shown excellent reproducibility and have been consistent with the known factors of loop operation. Although several problems (such as the offset in the potential of shielded electrodes, smaller offset voltages observed between the reference and working electrodes, and the establishment of accurate calibrations for the determination of corrosion products) remain to be solved, our experience gives us considerable confidence that this relatively simple transducer system can be widely applied to other test systems and ultimately to in-line reactor streams. The measurements also provide an explanation for the lower-than-expected results obtained in attempts to apply this technique to the hot-cell measurement of U(IV)/U(III) ratios in MSRE samples. Despite all our precautions, the contamination introduced during the sampling and transfer of the MSRE samples must have been at least equivalent to that observed during the introduction of corrosion specimens to the NCL-21 loop. It should be noted, however, that although these MSRE analyses provided little relevant data as to the ratio in the fuel, they indicated no evidence for interference to the method from the radioactivity of the samples.

# 10.2 THEORETICAL CONSIDERATIONS OF THE VOLTAMMETRIC IN-LINE DETERMINATION OF URANIUM(III)

J.M.Dale

The present method for determining the uranium(III) concentration in MSR fuel involves the measurement of the potential difference between the equilibrium potential of the melt and $E_{1/2}$ , the voltammetric equivalent of the standard potential of the U(IV)/U(III) couple. The equilibrium potential of the melt, $E_{\mathrm{EQ}}$ , is the potential of an inert platinum wire immersed in the melt and depends upon the U(IV)/U(III) ratio. The standard potential, $E_{1/2}$ , is the potential on the U(IV) reduction wave at which the concentrations of U(IV) and U(III) are equal. The relationship between $E_{\mathrm{EQ}}$ , $E_{1/2}$ , and U(IV)/U(III) is given by

$$
E _ {\mathrm {E Q}} = E _ {1 / 2} + \frac {R T}{n F} \ln \frac {\mathrm {U (I V)}}{\mathrm {U (I I I)}}.
$$

Because the voltammetric circuit uses $E_{\mathrm{EQ}}$ as a reference potential, we can consider it to be zero and

$$
- E _ {1 / 2} = \frac {R T}{n F} \ln \frac {\mathrm {U} (\mathrm {I V})}{\mathrm {U} (\mathrm {I I I})}.
$$

Evaluation of $E_{1/2}$ with respect to $E_{EQ}$ then permits the $\mathrm{U}(\mathrm{IV}) / \mathrm{U}(\mathrm{III})$ ratio to be calculated.

Nicholson and $\mathsf{Shain}^5$ have reported a solution to the boundary value problem that can be applied to this system. They found for a reversible couple where both species are soluble that $E_{1 / 2}$ corresponds to the potential on a theoretical reduction wave where the current is $85\%$ of the peak current. The experimental U(IV) reduction wave, however, from the NCL-21 thermal convection loop also involves the reduction of chromium, which could adversely affect the potential at which the $85\%$ point occurs. For this reason it was of interest to compare the theoretical reduction wave with the U(IV) reduction wave from the experimental system.

The potential, $E$ , of the working electrode during a voltammetric scan is represented by

$$
(E - E _ {1 / 2}) n = (R T / F) (\ln \gamma \theta - a t),
$$

where

$$
\begin{array}{l} \gamma = \sqrt {D _ {0} / D _ {r}} , \\ \theta = \exp \left[ \left(n F / R T\right) \left(E _ {i} - E _ {1 / 2}\right) \right], \\ a t = n F v t / R T. \\ \end{array}
$$

The term $\gamma \theta$ is a constant, where $D$ is the diffusion coefficient of the oxidized or reduced species and $E_{i}$ is the initial starting potential of the voltammetric scan. The term $at$ is a variable, where $\mathbf{v}$ is the voltage scan rate and $t$ is the time elapsed after the scan is started. All other terms have their usual significance. The current, $i$ , through the working electrode during a voltammetric scan is represented by

$$
i = n F A C _ {0} \sqrt {\pi D _ {0} a} \chi (a t).
$$

The term $\chi (at)$ is a function of $at$ , $A$ is the electrode area, and $C_0$ is the concentration of the U(IV) in the melt.

The expression

$$
\begin{array}{l} 2 \sqrt {\delta} \left\{\chi (1) \sqrt {k} + \sum_ {i = 1} ^ {k - 1} \sqrt {k - i} [ \chi (i + 1) - \chi (i) ] \right\} \\ = 1 / \left[ 1 + \gamma \theta \exp (- a t) \right], \\ \end{array}
$$

where $\delta = (at) / k$ , defines the relationship between the values of chi and the variable $at$ , where $k$ is the serial number of the particular value of chi being evaluated. In order to get a true theoretical current-potential curve it is necessary to solve for the values of chi at small voltage intervals, as each successive value of chi depends upon all of the previously determined values. A program was written for the PDP/81 for the numerical evaluation of the chi values over an 800-mV range at 2-mV intervals. This gave $\delta$ a value of 0.025. The initial potential, $E_{i}$ , was 500 mV anodic to $E_{1/2}$ , making $\ln \gamma \theta = 6.29$ .

Evaluation of the current expression, where $A = 0.418 \, \text{cm}^2$ , $C_0 = 0.282 \, \text{mole/liter}$ , $D_0 = 5.62 \times 10^{-6} \, \text{cm}^2 / \text{sec}$ , and $v = 0.1 \, \text{V/sec}$ , gives

$$
i = 5 3. 6 9 \chi (a t) m A
$$

for the potential defined by $at$ . The derivative current expression is

$$
d i / d t = \Delta i / \Delta t = 5 3. 6 9 \Delta \chi (a t) / \Delta t m A / \sec ,
$$

where the values of $\Delta x(at) / \Delta t$ are the incremental slopes between the $2\mathrm{-mV}$ intervals of the successive

![](images/1b69c0414bd116d78606ebad3aeb27742c0de44b5ff503923896aceb74f146e5.jpg)  
Fig. 10.5. Reduction of $\mathbf{U}(\mathbf{IV})$ in MSRE fuel.

values of chi. By proper substitution of the values of $\chi (at)$ and $\Delta \chi (at) / \Delta t$ , these two expressions were used for the construction of the theoretical current-potential curve and its derivative.

In Fig. 10.5 the experimental waves are represented by the solid lines, and the theoretical waves are represented by the points plotted at $10\mathrm{-mV}$ intervals. There is the expected deviation in the region where $\mathrm{Cr(II)}$ is reduced $(-0.4$ to $-0.5\mathrm{V})$ both on the normal and derivative waves. The experimental derivative peak is lower because the $\mathrm{Cr(II)}$ reduction causes the normal wave to rise less sharply in this region. However, the potentials at which the two derivatives show a maximum agree to within $5\mathrm{mV}$ and there is good agreement between the experimental and theoretical values of $E_{1 / 2}$ . It is concluded that the chromium reduction does not affect the current-potential curve for the U(IV) reduction at the $E_{1 / 2}$ potential and that the method for determining $E_{1 / 2}$ is valid.

# 10.3 ELECTROANALYTICAL STUDIES OF TITANIUM(IV) IN MOLTEN $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ZrF_4}$ (65.4-29.6-5.0 MOLE %)

F. R. Clayton<sup>6</sup>

D. L. Manning

Gleb Mamantov

Since reporting on the voltammetry and chronopotentiometry of titanium(IV) in molten LiF-NaF-KF, we have continued these studies in molten $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - }$ $\mathbf{ZrF_4}$ . The initial voltammetric studies were undertaken using $\mathrm{K}_2\mathrm{TiF}_6$ as the solute; however, the results of titanium(IV) reduction were greatly complicated by the

observed instability of Ti(IV) in the melt. White deposits collected in the cooler part of the electrolytic system after $\mathbf{K}_2\mathrm{TiF}_6$ was added to the melt. The volatilization of titanium tetrafluoride from the melt was confirmed in a separate experiment in a closed stainless steel vessel provided with a cold trap; the volatile product collected was identified as $\mathrm{TiF_4}$ by x-ray analysis.

Voltammetric studies of the oxidation of titanium(III) in molten $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ZrF_4}$ at $500^{\circ}$ were then initiated, since $\mathrm{TiF_3}$ was expected to be stable in the melt at this temperature. (The sublimation point of $\mathrm{TiF_3}$ is $930^{\circ}$ in vacuo.)

Well-defined linear-sweep voltammograms for molten $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ZrF_4}$ containing Ti(III) were obtained at a sheathed glassy carbon electrode. Similar but less-welldefined waves were obtained at an unsheathed platinum electrode. The wave corresponds to the anodic oxidation of Ti(III) to Ti(IV). This was the only wave observed upon addition of $\mathrm{TiF_3}$ within the potential limits of the melt, $+1.5$ to $-1.5\mathrm{V}$ measured at the platinum vs nickel(II)(saturated)/nickel reference electrode $(\mathrm{LaF}_3$ membrane type). These potential limits correspond to anodic dissolution of platinum and the reduction of $\mathrm{Zr(IV)}$ respectively. A plot of $i_p$ vs $\mathbf{v}^{1 / 2}$ resulted in a straight line which is indicative of a simple charge transfer reaction. The concentration dependence of $i_p$ was also linear.

The standard electrode potential $E^0$ for the $\mathrm{Ti(IV) / Ti(III)}$ couple may be estimated from the $E_{1 / 2}$ for the process $\mathrm{Ti(III)}\rightleftharpoons \mathrm{Ti(IV)} + e$ in molten $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ZrF_4}$ at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ . The $E_{1 / 2}$ is $+0.380\mathrm{V}$ with respect to the $\mathrm{Ni(II)(saturated) / Ni(II)}$ reference electrode. However, an extrapolation of a Nernstian plot of the nickel couple to unit mole fraction of nickel(II) gives a potential that is $183~\mathrm{mV}$ more anodic than the potential corresponding to the saturation point of nickel(II). Applying this correction of $-183~\mathrm{mV}$ to relate the potential of the reference electrode to the potential of a unit fraction $\mathrm{Ni(II) / Ni}$ couple, $E^0$ for the $\mathrm{Ti(IV) / Ti(III)}$ couple in molten $\mathrm{LiF - BeF_2 - ZrF_4}$ at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ is estimated at $+0.197\mathrm{V}$ (vs a unit mole fraction $\mathrm{Ni(II) / Ni}$ electrode).

The chronopotentiograms recorded at a sheathed glassy carbon electrode were reasonably well defined.

The product $i_0 \tau^{1/2}$ at current densities from 0.04 to 0.16 A/cm² was found to be reasonably constant at 0.060 ± 0.002 A sec¹² cm⁻². Transition times were in the range 0.1 to 2.3 sec.

Further verification of $n = 1$ for the Ti(III) oxidation was accomplished using the ratio of the voltammetric $i_{p} / \mathrm{v}^{1 / 2}$ to the chronopotentiometric $i_0\tau^{1 / 2}$ . This relationship for the determination of $n$ is given at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ as

$$
\frac {i _ {p} / \mathrm {v} ^ {1 / 2}}{i _ {0} \tau^ {1 / 2}} = 1. 9 6 n ^ {1 / 2}.
$$

An $n$ value of 0.9 was determined by this method, which is independent of other parameters such as concentration of the electroactive species, diffusion coefficient, and electrode area.

For chronopotentiometry, the half-wave potential for a reversible charge transfer corresponds to the potential at one-fourth the transition time. This value was found to be about $+0.40\mathrm{V}$ vs an $\mathrm{Ni(II)(saturated) / Ni}$ reference electrode, and agrees reasonably well with the $+0.380\mathrm{-V}$ voltammetric value. From our voltammetric and chronopotentiometric studies, we believe this electrode reaction conforms to a reversible $\mathrm{Ti(III)}\rightarrow \mathrm{Ti(IV)}$ oxidation process and that this reaction could be used to monitor any buildup of titanium(III) in a flowing salt stream.

# 10.4 ELECTROCHEMICAL STUDIES OF BISMUTH(III) IN MOLTEN LiF-BeF $_2$ -ZrF $_4$ AT $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$

J. S. Hammond<sup>9</sup>

D. L. Manning

Bismuth(III) being of importance in fuel stream purification systems is, therefore, a possible impurity in the reactor fuel salt. Voltammetric and chronopotentiometric studies were initiated to characterize the reduction behavior of this substance.

The linear sweep voltammograms were well defined for the reduction of bismuth at pyrolytic graphite, platinum, iridium, and silver indicator electrodes. Reverse scans indicated possibly alloy formation between bismuth and platinum and also between bismuth and silver. No evidence of alloy formation was observed at pyrolytic graphite and iridium electrodes. Of the indicator electrodes tested, the results were most reproducible at the iridium electrode. This material appears to be an excellent inert electrode for electroanalytical studies in corrosive melts.

The peak-shaped voltammogram corresponds to the reduction of $\mathrm{Bi}^{3+}$ to metallic bismuth at approximately $-0.05\mathrm{V}$ vs a platinum quasi-reference electrode. Plots of $i_p$ vs $\mathbf{v}^{1 / 2}$ were linear to about $5\mathrm{V / sec}$ , with slightly downward curvature at the faster scan rates. This suggests that the electrode reaction is becoming quasi-reversible at the faster scan rates. $^{10}$ The concentration dependence of $i_p$ was linear over the concentration range $\sim 10$ to $200~\mathrm{mM}$ Bi(III). It was also discovered that for appreciable periods of time (days), stable solutions of Bi(III) in molten LiF-BeF $_2$ -ZrF $_4$ could not be maintained in either graphite or copper cells. However, the bismuth solutions appeared to be more unstable in graphite. The mechanism for the bismuth instability is not yet resolved.

Chronopotentiograms recorded at an iridium electrode $(\sim 0.1~\mathrm{cm}^2)$ were well defined. The ratio of forward to reverse transition times was unity, indicating the reversible deposition of an insoluble substance. The values for the diffusion coefficient evaluated from voltammetry and chronopotentiometry were 1.08 and $1.03\times 10^{-6}\mathrm{cm}^2 /\mathrm{sec}$ respectively.

Verification of $n = 3$ for the bismuth reduction was carried out using the ratio of the voltammetric $i_{p} / \mathrm{v}^{1 / 2}$ to the chronopotentiometric $i_0\tau^{1 / 2}$ . This equation for the determination of $n$ at $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ is given as

$$
\frac {i _ {p} / \mathrm {v} ^ {1 / 2}}{i _ {0} \tau^ {1 / 2}} = 2. 6 9 n ^ {1 / 2}.
$$

An $n$ value of about 2.9 was obtained, which is independent of other parameters such as the concentration of the electroactive species, diffusion coefficients, and electrode area.

# 10.5 VOLTAMMETRY OF CHROMIUM(III) IN MOLTEN $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF (92-8 MOLE %)

D. L. Manning

A voltammetric study is under way on the reduction characteristics of chromium(III) added as $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{CrF}_6$ to molten $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ -NaF. The melt is contained in a graphite cell enclosed in a nickel apparatus to maintain an inert atmosphere. A cover gas of helium is maintained under static conditions at approximately 5 psi. Enough $\mathrm{Na}_3\mathrm{CrF}_6$ was added to give a chromium(III) concentration of approximately 410 ppm if all the reagent dissolved. Chemical analysis of melt samples taken at

440 and $500^{\circ}\mathrm{C}$ revealed a chromium(III) concentration of 200 and 300 ppm respectively.

The chromium reduction wave $(\mathrm{Cr}^{3+} \rightarrow \mathrm{Cr}^0)$ was observed at approximately $-1.0 \mathrm{~V}$ vs a platinum quasi-reference electrode at platinum and palladium indicator electrodes. The waves were reasonably well defined; limiting current values at platinum $(\sim 0.1 \mathrm{~cm}^2)$ were 500 and $1100 \mu \mathrm{A}$ at 440 and $500^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ , respectively, at a scan rate of $0.1 \mathrm{~V} / \mathrm{sec}$ . Scan rate studies revealed that plots of peak current $(\mu \mathrm{A})$ vs scan rate, $(\mathrm{V} / \mathrm{sec})^{1/2}$ , were linear to about $10 \mathrm{~V} / \mathrm{sec}$ . From the slope of the line, the diffusion coefficient of the $\mathrm{Cr(III)}$ species can be evaluated, and at $440^{\circ} \mathrm{C}$ was found to be about $2 \times 10^{-6} \mathrm{~cm}^2 / \mathrm{sec}$ .

Attempts to record chronopotentiograms were not successful; apparently the potential of the chromium reduction is too close to the melt limit to record meaningful transition times.

Additional experiments will be carried out to ascertain the linearity of the peak current vs chromium concentration plots.

# 10.6 VOLTAMMETRIC AND HYDROLYSIS STUDIES OF PROTONATED SPECIES IN MOLTEN $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$

D. L. Manning A. S. Meyer

We are continuing our investigation of the electrolysis of hydrogen from $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ melts at evacuated palladium electrodes. For the reduction of hydrogen, most electrode materials yield ill-defined and ragged voltammetric waves characteristic of gas film formation. However, at the palladium electrode the deposited hydrogen rapidly dissolves into the electrode to eliminate the film formation and gives well-defined voltammograms. Moreover, if the electrode is held at a sufficiently cathodic potential, some of the deposited hydrogen enters the evacuated portion of the electrode to yield a measurable pressure. Both of these techniques offer promise for a sensitive method for the in-line determination of protons in the coolant salt, with the pressure measurement technique offering the advantage of specificity.

During this period we have changed our experimental conditions by enclosing the melt in a nickel vessel as opposed to the quartz and Pyrex system that we usually use to protect molten fluoride salts. In both cases the actual melts are contained in a graphite liner under a static pressure of helium and make no direct contact

with the protective envelope. In contrast with the instability of the protonated species observed in the glass-enclosed systems, $^{12}$ the reduction waves in the metallic container were stable indefinitely in melts containing from 14 to $40~\mathrm{ppm}$ hydrogen, according to infrared analyses. The effect of glass containers on the stability of these melts is surprising, because the only communication to the container walls is through the gas phase. This behavior is consistent with the observations that the absorption peak of the $\mathrm{BF}_3\mathrm{OD}^-$ ion faded during measurement on melts contained in quartz cells. $^{13}$

We are now attempting to calibrate both of these types of measurements for analytical applications. This work is complicated by variations in the rate of diffusion through the electrode material and also by a low correlation of concentration between peak reduction currents, assuming a reasonable diffusion coefficient value and the analyses of the melts by the infrared pellet technique. Currently, we are investigating electrodes fabricated from silver-palladium alloys and are considering the possibility that the observed voltammetric wave results from the reduction of only one of multiple protonated species in equilibrium in the melt.

# 10.7 DETERMINATION OF HYDROGEN IN NaF-NaBF $_4$ SALTS

J.P.Young A.S.Meyer

Based on a calibration factor derived from an intimate physical mixture of $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ and $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ powders, $^{14}$ infrared (IR) determinations of protons, as $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ , in samples of $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ salts have been carried out by J. R. Lund (Analytical Chemistry Division). It can ordinarily be expected that standard additions of a species of interest to a sample matrix are a perfectly reliable way to generate samples for a calibration curve; however, the labile nature of $\mathrm{NaBF}_3\mathrm{OH}$ with respect to even moderate increases in temperature, $^{15}$ coupled with the expected instability of protons in any form in molten $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF_4}$ , $^{16}$ called for rather stringent tests to obtain independent proof that

the IR method gave reliable results. The first two reported attempts to verify the IR method by the mass spectral method using $\mathrm{D}_2$ as an isotopic diluent $^{14}$ were subject to an exorbitant blank which resulted from $\mathrm{D}_2$ exchange with $\mathrm{OH}^-$ in the Pyrex experimental apparatus. Some or all of the measured hydrogen attributed to the sample may have resulted from increased exchange of the $\mathrm{OH}^-$ in the Pyrex ampuls due to etching. The only eutectic salt samples available at that time contained 20 to 30 ppm protons (IR); the only significantly lower proton concentration was in a sample of recrystallized $\mathrm{NaBF}_4$ . It is conceivable that a fortuitous difference in etching rates between the two samples could have yielded the excellent agreement obtained. During this period these Pyrex blanks have been reduced from 8 micromoles in a typical sample to 4 micromoles of $\mathrm{H}_2$ (these values correspond to 10 and 5 ppm protons respectively). This reduction in blanks was accomplished by prolonged heating of the Pyrex ampuls at a temperature near their softening point in a stream of helium prior to the addition of any sample. Data collected on the correlation of mass spectral and IR determinations of protons in $\mathrm{NaF - NaBF}_4$ samples are given in Table 10.1.

Most of the results of this comparison are in acceptable agreement between the two methods. Occasional high results by the mass spectral method are not unexpected in view of the experimental difficulties of handling of samples and ampuls. Moreover, no systematic variation is observed with improvements in the blank. We therefore conclude that the results of the IR analyses do indicate that a significant concentration of hydrogen is present in the samples submitted from corrosion loops. Precise confirmation of the IR calibra

Table 10.1. Determination of hydrogen in $\mathrm{NaBF_4}$ samples by infrared and mass spectral methods
